<div style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0"><img alt="Circle frame.svg" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/200px-Circle_frame.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/300px-Circle_frame.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/400px-Circle_frame.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Self-reported ethnic origins of Canadians (2011 census)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnic2011d_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ethnic2011d-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Blue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Canadian" title="European Canadian">European</a> (76.7%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Gray; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_Canadian" title="Asian Canadian">Asian</a> (14.2%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Canada" title="Indigenous peoples in Canada">Indigenous</a> (4.3%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Green; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Black_Canadians" title="Black Canadians">Black</a> (2.9%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American_Canadian" title="Latin American Canadian">Latin American</a> (1.2%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_origins_of_people_in_Canada" title="Ethnic origins of people in Canada">Multiracial</a> (0.5%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:White; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_origins_of_people_in_Canada" title="Ethnic origins of people in Canada">Other</a> (0.3%)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Canada_2006_Census" title="Canada 2006 Census">2006 census</a>, the country's largest <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_origins_of_people_in_Canada" title="Ethnic origins of people in Canada">self-reported ethnic origin</a> is Canadian (accounting for 32% of the population), followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Canadian" title="English Canadian">English</a> (21%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Canadian" title="French Canadian">French</a> (15.8%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Canadian" title="Scottish Canadian">Scottish</a> (15.1%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_Canadian" title="Irish Canadian">Irish</a> (13.9%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadians_of_German_ethnicity" title="Canadians of German ethnicity">German</a> (10.2%), <a href="/wiki/Italian_Canadians" title="Italian Canadians">Italian</a> (4.6%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Canadian" title="Chinese Canadian">Chinese</a> (4.3%), <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> (4.0%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Canadian" title="Ukrainian Canadian">Ukrainian</a> (3.9%), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadians_of_Dutch_descent" title="Canadians of Dutch descent">Dutch</a> (3.3%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> There are 600 recognized <a href="/wiki/List_of_First_Nations_peoples" title="List of First Nations peoples">First Nations governments or bands</a>, encompassing a total of 1,172,790 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Canada's indigenous population is growing at almost twice the national rate, and four percent of Canada's population claimed an indigenous identity in 2006. Another 16.2 percent of the population belonged to a non-indigenous <a href="/wiki/Visible_minority" title="Visible minority">visible minority</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> In 2006, the largest visible minority groups were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asian_Canadian" title="South Asian Canadian">South Asian</a> (4.0%), Chinese (3.9%) and <a href="/wiki/Black_Canadians" title="Black Canadians">Black</a> (2.5%). Between 2001 and 2006, the visible minority population rose by 27.2 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> In 1961, less than two percent of Canada's population (about 300,000 people) were members of visible minority groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> By 2007, almost one in five (19.8%) were foreign-born, with nearly 60 percent of new immigrants coming from Asia (including the Middle East).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> The leading sources of immigrants to Canada were China, the Philippines and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> According to Statistics Canada, visible minority groups could account for a third of the Canadian population by 2031.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Canada" title="Religion in Canada">Religion in Canada</a></div>
<p>Canada is religiously diverse, encompassing a wide range of beliefs and customs. Canada has no official church, and the government is officially committed to <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">religious pluralism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon2008_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moon2008-256">[256]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_Canada" title="Freedom of religion in Canada">Freedom of religion in Canada</a> is a constitutionally protected right, allowing individuals to assemble and worship without limitation or interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2012n_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2012n-257">[257]</a></sup> The practice of religion is now generally considered a private matter throughout society and the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoyleSheen2013_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoyleSheen2013-258">[258]</a></sup> With Christianity in decline after having once been central and integral to Canadian culture and daily life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts2005w_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts2005w-259">[259]</a></sup> Canada has become a <a href="/wiki/Postchristianity" title="Postchristianity">post-Christian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secular</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BramadatSeljak2009_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-BramadatSeljak2009-260">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen2004_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen2004-261">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GregoryJohnston2009_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-GregoryJohnston2009-262">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BermanBhargava2013b_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-BermanBhargava2013b-263">[263]</a></sup> The majority of Canadians consider <a href="/wiki/Importance_of_religion_by_country" title="Importance of religion by country">religion to be unimportant</a> in their daily lives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Punnett2015a_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Punnett2015a-264">[264]</a></sup> but still believe in God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haskell2009_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haskell2009-265">[265]</a></sup> According to the 2011 census, 67.3% of Canadians identify as Christian; of these, Roman Catholics make up the largest group, accounting for 38.7% of the population. Much of the remainder is made up of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a>, who accounted for approximately 27% in a 2011 survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011NHSreligiondetailed_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011NHSreligiondetailed-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> The largest Protestant <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denomination</a> is the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Canada" title="United Church of Canada">United Church of Canada</a> (accounting for 6.1% of Canadians), followed by <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church_of_Canada" title="Anglican Church of Canada">Anglicans</a> (5.0%), and <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_Canada" title="Baptists in Canada">Baptists</a> (1.9%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statcan1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-statcan1-3">[3]</a></sup> Secularization has been growing since the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> In 2011, 23.9% declared <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">no religious affiliation</a>, compared to 16.5% in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> The remaining 8.8% are affiliated with non-Christian religions, the largest of which are <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Canada" title="Islam in Canada">Islam</a> (3.2%) and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Canada" title="Hinduism in Canada">Hinduism</a> (1.5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statcan1_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-statcan1-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Canada" title="Languages of Canada">Languages of Canada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg"><img alt="Map of Canada with English speakers and French speakers at a percentage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1100" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg/220px-Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg/330px-Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg/440px-Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilinguisme_au_Canada-fr.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Approximately 98% of Canadians can speak English and/or French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Highlights_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Highlights-271">[271]</a></sup>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><b><small><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFE400; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> English – 56.9%</small></b></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><b><small><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #D8A820; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> English and French (Bilingual) – 16.1%</small></b></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><b><small><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B07400; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> French – 21.3%</small></b></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><b><small><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F5F5DC; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Sparsely populated area (</small></b> <small>&lt; <b>0.4 persons per km<sup>2</sup>)</b></small></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A multitude of languages are used by Canadians, with <a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">French</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a>) being the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">mother tongues</a> of approximately 60% and 20% of Canadians, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Highlightsb_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Highlightsb-272">[272]</a></sup> Nearly 6.8 million Canadians listed a non-official language as their mother tongue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> Some of the most common non-official first languages include <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Cantonese</a>; 1,072,555 first-language speakers), <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> (430,705), <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> (410,670), <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (409,200), and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> (407,490).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Canada's federal government practices <a href="/wiki/Official_bilingualism_in_Canada" title="Official bilingualism in Canada">official bilingualism</a>, which is applied by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Commissioner_of_Official_Languages" title="Office of the Commissioner of Official Languages">Commissioner of Official Languages</a> in consonance with <a href="/wiki/Section_16_of_the_Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Section 16 of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Section 16 of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a> and the Federal <a href="/wiki/Official_Languages_Act_(Canada)" title="Official Languages Act (Canada)">Official Languages Act</a> English and French have equal status in federal courts, parliament, and in all federal institutions. Citizens have the right, where there is sufficient demand, to receive federal government services in either English or French and official-<a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">language minorities</a> are guaranteed their own schools in all provinces and territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1977 <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_French_Language" title="Charter of the French Language">Charter of the French Language</a> established French as the official language of Quebec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> Although more than 85 percent of French-speaking Canadians live in Quebec, there are substantial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francophone" title="Francophone">Francophone</a> populations in <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_New_Brunswick" title="Demographics of New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Albertan" title="Franco-Albertan">Alberta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Franco-Manitoban" title="Franco-Manitoban">Manitoba</a>; <a href="/wiki/Franco-Ontarian" title="Franco-Ontarian">Ontario</a> has the largest French-speaking population outside Quebec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webber2015_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webber2015-277">[277]</a></sup> New Brunswick, the only officially bilingual province, has a French-speaking Acadian minority constituting 33 percent of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auer2010b_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auer2010b-278">[278]</a></sup> There are also clusters of Acadians in southwestern Nova Scotia, on Cape Breton Island, and through central and western Prince Edward Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other provinces have no official languages as such, but French is used as a language of instruction, in courts, and for other government services, in addition to English. Manitoba, Ontario, and Quebec allow for both English and French to be spoken in the provincial legislatures, and laws are enacted in both languages. In Ontario, French has some legal status, but is not fully co-official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> There are 11 <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Canada#Aboriginal_languages" title="Languages of Canada">indigenous language groups</a>, composed of more than 65 distinct languages and dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> Of these, only the <a href="/wiki/Cree_language" title="Cree language">Cree</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_language" title="Inuit language">Inuktitut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Ojibway</a> languages have a large enough population of fluent speakers to be considered viable to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_extinction" title="Language extinction">survive in the long term</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> Several indigenous languages have official status in the Northwest Territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> Inuktitut is the majority language in Nunavut, and is one of three official languages in the territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, Canada is home to many <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign languages</a>, some of which are Indigenous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">American Sign Language</a> (ASL) is spoken across the country due to the prevalence of ASL in primary and secondary schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JepsenClerck2015_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-JepsenClerck2015-286">[286]</a></sup> Due to its historical relation to the francophone culture, <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Sign_Language" title="Quebec Sign Language">Quebec Sign Language</a> (LSQ) is spoken primarily in Quebec, although there are sizeable Francophone communities in New Brunswick, Ontario and Manitoba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaileyDolby2002_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaileyDolby2002-287">[287]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Canada" title="Culture of Canada">Culture of Canada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg"><img alt="Bill Reid's 1980 sculpture Raven and The First Men. Raven crushing men under turtle shell" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg/220px-Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg/330px-Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg/440px-Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raven-and-the-first-men.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bill_Reid" title="Bill Reid">Bill Reid</a>'s 1980 sculpture <i>Raven and The First Men</i>. The raven is a figure common to many of Canada's indigenous mythologies</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Canada's culture draws influences from its broad range of constituent nationalities, and policies that promote a "<a href="/wiki/Just_society" title="Just society">just society</a>" are constitutionally protected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman2012_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newman2012-289">[289]</a></sup> Canada has placed emphasis on equality and inclusiveness for all its people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuoWong2015_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuoWong2015-290">[290]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism_in_Canada" title="Multiculturalism in Canada">Multiculturalism</a> is often cited as one of Canada's significant accomplishments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sikka2014v_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sikka2014v-291">[291]</a></sup> and a key distinguishing element of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_identity" title="Canadian identity">Canadian identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polls_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-polls-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caplow2001a_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caplow2001a-293">[293]</a></sup> In Quebec, cultural identity is strong, and many commentators speak of a <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Quebec" title="Culture of Quebec">culture of Quebec</a> that is distinct from English Canadian culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> However, as a whole, Canada is in theory a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_mosaic" title="Cultural mosaic">cultural mosaic</a>—a collection of regional ethnic subcultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p>Canada's approach to governance emphasizing multiculturalism, which is based on selective <a href="/wiki/Economic_migrant" title="Economic migrant">immigration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_integration" title="Social integration">social integration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suppression_of_dissent" title="Suppression of dissent">suppression</a> of far-right politics, has wide public support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrosea_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrosea-296">[296]</a></sup> Government policies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_care_in_Canada" title="Health care in Canada">publicly funded health care</a>, <a href="/wiki/Income_taxes_in_Canada" title="Income taxes in Canada">higher taxation</a> to <a href="/wiki/Canadian_federal_budget" title="Canadian federal budget">redistribute wealth</a>, the outlawing of <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_Canada" title="Capital punishment in Canada">capital punishment</a>, strong efforts to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Poverty_in_Canada" title="Poverty in Canada">poverty</a>, strict <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gun_politics_in_Canada" title="Gun politics in Canada">gun control</a>, and the legalization of <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Canada" title="Same-sex marriage in Canada">same-sex marriage</a> are further social indicators of Canada's political and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_values" title="Canadian values">cultural values</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup> Canadians also identify with the country's health care institutions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_peacekeeping" title="Canadian peacekeeping">peacekeeping</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Parks_of_Canada" title="National Parks of Canada">National park system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polls_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-polls-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, Canada has been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">British</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_culture" title="French culture">French</a>, and indigenous cultures and traditions. Through their language, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_art" title="Native American art">art</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Nations_music" title="First Nations music">music</a>, Indigenous peoples continue to influence the Canadian identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> During the 20th century, Canadians with African, Caribbean and Asian nationalities have added to the Canadian identity and its culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TetteyPuplampu2005_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-TetteyPuplampu2005-301">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canadian_humour" title="Canadian humour">Canadian humour</a> is an integral part of the Canadian identity and is reflected in its <a href="/wiki/Canadian_folklore" title="Canadian folklore">folklore</a>, literature, music, art and media. The primary characteristics of Canadian humour are irony, parody, and satire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nieguth2015_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nieguth2015-302">[302]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_comedians" title="List of Canadian comedians">Canadian comedians</a> have archived international success in the American TV and film industries and are amongst the most recognized in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeYork2016_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeeYork2016-303">[303]</a></sup></p>
<p>Canada has a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Canada" title="Media of Canada">media sector</a>, but its cultural output; particularly in <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Canada" title="Cinema of Canada">English films</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television_in_Canada" title="Television in Canada">television shows</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_magazines" title="List of Canadian magazines">magazines</a>, is often overshadowed by imports from the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vipond2011y_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vipond2011y-304">[304]</a></sup> As a result, the preservation of a distinctly Canadian culture is supported by federal government programs, laws, and institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">Canadian Broadcasting Corporation</a> (CBC), the <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Board_of_Canada" title="National Film Board of Canada">National Film Board of Canada</a> (NFB), and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Radio-television_and_Telecommunications_Commission" title="Canadian Radio-television and Telecommunications Commission">Canadian Radio-television and Telecommunications Commission</a> (CRTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwardson2008c_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwardson2008c-305">[305]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_symbols_of_Canada" title="National symbols of Canada">National symbols of Canada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beaver_sculpture,_Centre_Block.jpg"><img alt="The mother beaver sculpture outside the House of Commons" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2066" data-file-width="2298" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg/220px-Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg/330px-Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg/440px-Beaver_sculpture%2C_Centre_Block.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beaver_sculpture,_Centre_Block.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The mother beaver</i> on the Canadian parliament's <a href="/wiki/Peace_Tower" title="Peace Tower">Peace Tower</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> The five flowers on the shield each represent an ethnicity: <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a>: <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleur_de_lis" title="Fleur de lis">Fleur de lis</a>: <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>; <a href="/wiki/Thistle" title="Thistle">thistle</a>: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>; <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrock</a>: <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a>: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Canada's national symbols are influenced by natural, historical, and indigenous sources. The use of the <a href="/wiki/Maple_leaf" title="Maple leaf">maple leaf</a> as a Canadian symbol dates to the early 18th century. The maple leaf is depicted on Canada's <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Canada" title="Flag of Canada">current</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Red_Ensign" title="Canadian Red Ensign">previous flags</a>, and on the <a href="/wiki/Arms_of_Canada" title="Arms of Canada">Arms of Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbol1_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-symbol1-307">[307]</a></sup> The Arms of Canada is closely modelled after the <a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom">royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom</a> with French and distinctive Canadian elements replacing or added to those derived from the British version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gough2010g_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gough2010g-308">[308]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_Canada" title="Great Seal of Canada">Great Seal of Canada</a> is a governmental <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seal</a> used for purposes of state, being set on <a href="/wiki/Letters_patent" title="Letters patent">letters patent</a>, proclamations and commissions, for representatives of the Queen and for the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Canada" title="Cabinet of Canada">cabinet ministers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_governor_(Canada)" title="Lieutenant governor (Canada)">lieutenant governors</a>, senators, and judges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formal_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-formal-309">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> Other prominent symbols include the <a href="/wiki/Beaver" title="Beaver">beaver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada_goose" title="Canada goose">Canada goose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Common_loon" title="Common loon">common loon</a>, the Crown, the Royal Canadian Mounted Police,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbol1_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-symbol1-307">[307]</a></sup> and more recently the <a href="/wiki/Totem_pole" title="Totem pole">totem pole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuksuk" title="Inuksuk">Inuksuk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_Canadian_dollar" title="Coins of the Canadian dollar">Canadian coins</a> feature many of these symbols: the loon on the <a href="/wiki/Loonie" title="Loonie">$1 coin</a>, the Arms of Canada on the <a href="/wiki/50-cent_piece_(Canadian_coin)" title="50-cent piece (Canadian coin)">50¢ piece</a>, the beaver on the <a href="/wiki/Nickel_(Canadian_coin)" title="Nickel (Canadian coin)">nickel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berman2008_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2008-312">[312]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Penny_(Canadian_coin)" title="Penny (Canadian coin)">penny</a>, removed from circulation in 2013, featured the maple leaf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> The Queen' s image appears on <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">$20 bank notes</a>, and on the obverse of all current Canadian coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berman2008_312-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2008-312">[312]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_literature" title="Canadian literature">Canadian literature</a></div>
<p>Canadian literature is often divided into French- and English-language literatures, which are rooted in the literary traditions of France and Britain, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup> There are four major themes that can be found within historical Canadian literature; nature, frontier life, Canada's position within the world, all three of which tie into the <a href="/wiki/Garrison_mentality" title="Garrison mentality">garrison mentality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup> By the 1990s, Canadian literature was viewed as some of the world's best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dominic2010_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dominic2010-316">[316]</a></sup> Canada's ethnic and cultural diversity are reflected in its literature, with many of its most prominent modern writers focusing on ethnic life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup> Arguably, the best-known living Canadian writer internationally (especially since the deaths of <a href="/wiki/Robertson_Davies" title="Robertson Davies">Robertson Davies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Richler" title="Mordecai Richler">Mordecai Richler</a>) is <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a>, a prolific novelist, poet, and literary critic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup> Numerous other <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_writers" title="List of Canadian writers">Canadian authors</a> have accumulated international literary awards;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Alice_Munro" title="Alice Munro">Alice Munro</a>, who has been called the best living writer of short stories in English;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Booker_Prize" title="Booker Prize">Booker Prize</a> recipient <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ondaatje" title="Michael Ondaatje">Michael Ondaatje</a>, who is perhaps best known for the novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_English_Patient" title="The English Patient">The English Patient</a></i>, which was adapted as a <a href="/wiki/The_English_Patient_(film)" title="The English Patient (film)">film of the same name</a> that won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Academy Award for Best Picture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_art" title="Canadian art">Canadian art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Jack_Pine,_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg"><img alt="Oil on canvas painting of a tree dominating its rocky landscape during a sunset" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="640" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg/220px-The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg/330px-The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg/440px-The_Jack_Pine%2C_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Jack_Pine,_by_Tom_Thomson.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Jack_Pine" title="The Jack Pine">The Jack Pine</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Thomson" title="Tom Thomson">Tom Thomson</a>. Oil on canvas, 1916, in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Canada" title="National Gallery of Canada">National Gallery of Canada</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Canadian visual art has been dominated by figures such as <a href="/wiki/Tom_Thomson" title="Tom Thomson">Tom Thomson</a> – the country's most famous painter – and by the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven_(artists)" title="Group of Seven (artists)">Group of Seven</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> Thomson's career painting Canadian landscapes spanned a decade up to his death in 1917 at age 39.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup> The Group were painters with a nationalistic and idealistic focus, who first exhibited their distinctive works in May 1920. Though referred to as having seven members, five artists—<a href="/wiki/Lawren_Harris" title="Lawren Harris">Lawren Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/A._Y._Jackson" title="A. Y. Jackson">A. Y. Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lismer" title="Arthur Lismer">Arthur Lismer</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._E._H._MacDonald" title="J. E. H. MacDonald">J. E. H. MacDonald</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Varley" title="Frederick Varley">Frederick Varley</a>—were responsible for articulating the Group's ideas. They were joined briefly by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Johnston_(artist)" title="Frank Johnston (artist)">Frank Johnston</a>, and by commercial artist <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Carmichael" title="Franklin Carmichael">Franklin Carmichael</a>. <a href="/wiki/A._J._Casson" title="A. J. Casson">A. J. Casson</a> became part of the Group in 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup> Associated with the Group was another prominent Canadian artist, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Carr" title="Emily Carr">Emily Carr</a>, known for her landscapes and portrayals of the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Pacific_Northwest_Coast" title="Indigenous peoples of the Pacific Northwest Coast">Indigenous peoples of the Pacific Northwest Coast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> Since the 1950s, works of <a href="/wiki/Inuit_art" title="Inuit art">Inuit art</a> have been given as gifts to foreign dignitaries by the Canadian government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Canada" title="Music of Canada">Music of Canada</a></div>
<p>The Canadian music industry is the sixth-largest in the world producing internationally renowned <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_composers" title="List of Canadian composers">composers</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_musicians" title="List of Canadian musicians">musicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_bands_from_Canada" title="List of bands from Canada">ensembles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HullHutchison2011a_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-HullHutchison2011a-327">[327]</a></sup> Music broadcasting in the country is regulated by the CRTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AchesonMaule2009_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-AchesonMaule2009-328">[328]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Academy_of_Recording_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Canadian Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences">Canadian Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences</a> presents Canada's music industry awards, the <a href="/wiki/Juno_Award" title="Juno Award">Juno Awards</a>, which were first awarded in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Music_Hall_of_Fame" title="Canadian Music Hall of Fame">Canadian Music Hall of Fame</a> established in 1976 honours Canadian musicians for their lifetime achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmann2004_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffmann2004-330">[330]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anthems_and_nationalistic_songs_of_Canada" title="Anthems and nationalistic songs of Canada">Patriotic music in Canada</a> dates back over 200 years as a distinct category from British patriotism, preceding the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1867" title="Constitution Act, 1867">first legal steps to independence</a> by over 50 years. The earliest, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bold_Canadian" title="The Bold Canadian">The Bold Canadian</a></i>, was written in 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup> The national anthem of Canada, "<a href="/wiki/O_Canada" title="O Canada">O Canada</a>", was originally commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Quebec" title="Lieutenant Governor of Quebec">Lieutenant Governor of Quebec</a>, the Honourable <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9odore_Robitaille" title="Théodore Robitaille">Théodore Robitaille</a>, for the 1880 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F%C3%AAte_nationale_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Fête nationale du Québec">St. Jean-Baptiste Day</a> ceremony, and was officially adopted in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calixa_Lavall%C3%A9e" title="Calixa Lavallée">Calixa Lavallée</a> wrote the music, which was a setting of a patriotic poem composed by the poet and judge Sir <a href="/wiki/Adolphe-Basile_Routhier" title="Adolphe-Basile Routhier">Adolphe-Basile Routhier</a>. The text was originally only in French, before it was translated to English in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Canada" title="Sport in Canada">Sport in Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Canadian_sports" title="History of Canadian sports">History of Canadian sports</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg"><img alt="Hockey players and fans celebrating" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg/220px-Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg/330px-Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg/440px-Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canada2010WinterOlympicsOTcelebration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Canada's <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> victory at the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a> in Vancouver</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Canadian_sports" title="History of Canadian sports">roots of organized sports in Canada</a> date back to the 1770s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup> Canada's official national sports are <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">Golf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badminton" title="Badminton">badminton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cycling" title="Cycling">cycling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swimming_(sport)" title="Swimming (sport)">swimming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowling" title="Bowling">bowling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canoeing" title="Canoeing">canoeing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a> and the study of <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a> are widely enjoyed at the youth and amateur levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup></p>
<p>Canada shares several <a href="/wiki/Major_professional_sports_leagues_in_the_United_States_and_Canada" title="Major professional sports leagues in the United States and Canada">major professional sports leagues</a> with the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ButenkoGil-Lafuente2010_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-ButenkoGil-Lafuente2010-337">[337]</a></sup> Canadian teams in these leagues include seven franchises in the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a>, as well as three <a href="/wiki/Soccer_in_Canada#Major_League_Soccer" title="Soccer in Canada">Major League Soccer</a> teams and one team in each of <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a>. Other popular professional sports in Canada include <a href="/wiki/Canadian_football" title="Canadian football">Canadian football</a>, which is played in the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Football_League" title="Canadian Football League">Canadian Football League</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Lacrosse_League" title="National Lacrosse League">National Lacrosse League</a> lacrosse, and <a href="/wiki/Curling" title="Curling">curling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorrowWamsley2016_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorrowWamsley2016-338">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>Canada has participated in almost every Olympic Games since <a href="/wiki/Canada_at_the_1900_Summer_Olympics" title="Canada at the 1900 Summer Olympics">its Olympic debut in 1900</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MallonHeijmans2011_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-MallonHeijmans2011-339">[339]</a></sup> and has hosted several high-profile international sporting events, including the <a href="/wiki/1976_Summer_Olympics" title="1976 Summer Olympics">1976 Summer Olympics</a> in Montreal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell2009_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howell2009-340">[340]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/1988_Winter_Olympics" title="1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Winter Olympics</a> in Calgary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorneWhannel2016_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorneWhannel2016-341">[341]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1994_Basketball_World_Championship" title="1994 Basketball World Championship">1994 Basketball World Championship</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blevins2012_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blevins2012-342">[342]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/2007_FIFA_U-20_World_Cup" title="2007 FIFA U-20 World Cup">2007 FIFA U-20 World Cup</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParentChappelet2015_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParentChappelet2015-343">[343]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a> in Vancouver,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Development2006_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-Development2006-344">[344]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Whistler,_British_Columbia" title="Whistler, British Columbia">Whistler, British Columbia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fromm2006_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fromm2006-345">[345]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/2015_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2015 FIFA Women's World Cup">2015 FIFA Women's World Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup></p>
